Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 2
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day contains a contiguous block of exact zero readings from approximately 01:00 to 07:15 (25 consecutive zeros), while the rest of the day shows reduced but non-zero consumption. This matches Attack 2 (on‑off attack) defined as replacing samples with zeros for a random duration. Attack IEEE was ruled out because it would scatter zeros across the whole day, not produce a single long zero block. Attack 1 (constant scaling) would not produce any exact zero values. The pattern of a sustained zero period followed by a return to measurable consumption is a textbook example of an on‑off attack.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: A contiguous segment of 15‑minute readings is replaced with zeros, while the remaining intervals contain reduced (but not zero) values. The zero block interrupts the typical morning ramp‑up seen in the reference week (e.g., Day 5 reference values in the same time window are 100–165).
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Overall daily mean drops from ~167.5 to 46.35 (a 72% reduction). The trend is broken: the normal morning increase is replaced by a flat zero period, and the subsequent recovery reaches only about half the normal peak (~133 vs ~270). The characteristic shape is lost.
- Possible technical causes: Temporary full meter bypass (e.g., physical tampering or magnetic interference), false data injection that commands the meter to report zero for a chosen period, or reverse current that forces the meter to register no energy flow during the attack interval.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. Reasoning content 
We are given reference week data for a smart meter and a query day. The query day has dramatically lower consumption: min=0, max=129.03, mean=7.06, compared to reference week days with means around 180-240. The pattern is mostly zeros with occasional small spikes. This looks clearly manipulated.

Check the query day data: starts with zeros for many intervals, then a few small values (2.86, 12.21), back to zeros, then later a spike up to 129.03, then back down. The day is predominantly zeros, but not entirely zero. So it's not Attack 0 (all zeros). The mean is very low. The pattern is not consistent with the typical daily profile which shows higher consumption during evening/night and lower at night, with peaks around 300-400. Here, it's mostly zero with a brief spike.

This resembles an on-off attack (Attack 2) where zeros are placed for random durations, but note there's a spike of normal-looking values. Attack 2 says "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration". That could mean parts of the day are zero, but other parts are actual. In the query day, there is a segment of non-zero values that seem plausible (maybe real consumption?). Actually, the spike: 30.90, 44.92, 100.99, 129.03, 54.27, 68.28, 35.57, 44.92, 68.28, 30.90, 16.88, 21.55, then zeros. Those values are not extremely high like the reference peaks (300+), but they are higher than the average of the reference? The reference peaks are higher, but the magnitude of this spike is still plausible for a lower consumption period? On the reference days, there are intervals with values in the 100-200 range frequently. The spike here reaches 129.03, which is about half the typical peak. Attack 2 says "changes both trend and amplitude". That fits: zeros interspersed with real (or reduced?) consumption.

But also consider Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." That's similar: some intervals real, some zero. The query day seems to have many zeros and some non-zero intervals that could be actual readings. So either Attack 2 or Attack IEEE could be the cause. The description of Attack 2: "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack." That implies contiguous blocks of zeros. In the query data, the zeros are in contiguous blocks: the first many intervals are zero, then a brief non-zero, then zeros again, then a spike, then zeros again. So it's on-off with random durations. Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day." That could also produce a pattern like this. The difference is that Attack 2 replaces "consumption samples with zeros for a random duration" implying contiguous zero blocks, while Attack IEEE operates per interval, which could be independent. The given query day has blocks of zeros, so Attack 2 seems more fitting.

Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." That would not necessarily set to zero; it says reduced. And there would be a continuous period of reduction. Here we have zeros, not just reduced, and multiple blocks.

Attack 1: multiplies by constant factor <1; would preserve shape, not produce zeros. Not this.

Attack 3: multiplies by different random factors per sample; would produce reduced, non-zero values, not zeros.

Attack 5: multiplies average normal by random (0,1) per sample; would produce values around half of average (since mean is ~210, half would be ~100). The query day mean is 7, not 100. So not Attack 5.

Attack 6: cut-off above a ceiling; would cap values, not zero them.

Attack 7: subtract a cut-off; would reduce but not produce many zeros.

Attack 9: all replaced by average daily; would be constant, not zero.

Attack 10: reverses pattern; not zero.

Attack 12: replace with lower consumer pattern; would not have zeros.

So the main candidates are Attack 2 and Attack IEEE. Attack 2 explicitly says "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration" which exactly matches the presence of zero blocks and real (or nearly real) consumption interspersed. The data shows long blocks of zeros, then a block of non-zero values, then zeros again. The non-zero block could be real consumption. So I'll choose Attack 2.

Confidence: High, because the pattern is classic on-off.

This reduces total consumption magnitude, disrupts the normal trend (missing usage during typical high-consumption periods), and can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, or reverse current.

The non‑zero values are much lower than the reference Friday’s pattern, but the defining manipulation is the presence of these extended zero intervals. Attack 3 (multiplicative random factors per sample) was considered because it could produce zeros and scaled non‑zero values, but it is unlikely to generate such long, uninterrupted zero blocks by chance. Attack IEEE (per‑interval random zeroing) was also considered, but the probability of randomly obtaining 40 consecutive zeros is negligible; the structured zero blocks strongly indicate a duration‑based on‑off attack.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:  
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: The attacker replaces the true consumption with zero for randomly selected continuous periods. During the “on” intervals, the meter may report actual or possibly reduced consumption (the non‑zero values seen are below typical Friday levels).  
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Average consumption collapses from ~111 to ~5, and large segments of the daily trend become flat at zero, destroying the normal morning/evening peak pattern.  
- Possible technical causes: Full meter bypass (e.g., physically disconnecting the meter), false data injection that forces zero records, reverse current causing the meter to stop, or temporary deactivation via a switch or magnetic interference.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Original | Manipulated

Identified Attack:  (fill only if manipulated)
- Most Likely: Attack X
- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes

Typically, in an on-off attack, the attacker completely bypasses the meter for certain intervals (reporting zero) and for other intervals the meter works normally. But here, the non-zero intervals are also much lower. Could it be that the attacker is only turning on some appliances during those intervals? Or maybe the query day is from a different season or something? The metadata says it's the same month (October) but dom=13 vs reference week dom=0..6? Wait, reference week Day 0 dom=0, Day 1 dom=1, Day 2 dom=2, Day 3 dom=3, Day 4 dom=4, Day 5 dom=5, Day 6 dom=6. Query day dom=13. So it's a different day of the month (13th vs 6th). It's still October, same season, same weekday (Friday). So consumption pattern could be similar, but not identical. However, the magnitude difference is extreme: mean 4.97 vs 111.72. That's a 95.5% reduction. Could it be that the customer had almost no consumption that day? Unlikely, given the reference week shows consistent consumption around 100-116. So it's definitely manipulated.

Given the presence of many zeros and reduced non-zero values, the attack that best matches is Attack 3: "The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." This would produce values that are all scaled, including many that are zero if the factor is zero? But the definition says "multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1" - typically that implies continuous random factors, possibly including 0 but not necessarily a high probability of exact zero. However, in this query, a large number of samples are exactly zero (many in a row). It would be improbable to get exactly zero from a continuous random multiplier unless the factor is exactly 0, which could happen if the attacker sets it to zero intentionally. But the attack description says "different random factors per sample" - it doesn't specify distribution. It could be that the factors are chosen such that some are zero. Attack 3 is essentially a multiplicative attack with varying factor. In contrast, Attack 2 is specifically zeroing for random durations. The query has long contiguous blocks of zeros (first 40 intervals, then later 17 intervals, and some scattered zeros). That matches "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration" because the zero blocks are contiguous durations. However, the non-zero intervals are not simply the original values; they are also manipulated. Could it be that the zero durations are also used to turn off the meter, and when it's on, the attacker also applies some reduction? That might be a mixed attack not covered. But among the given attacks, Attack 2 is the closest because the defining characteristic is the presence of zero blocks. Attack 3 would likely produce fewer exact zeros unless the random factor is often zero, but then the non-zero values would be original * factor, and the distribution of factors wouldn't create long zero blocks unless the factor is exactly zero for many consecutive samples. Attack 2 explicitly says "for a random duration" implying consecutive zeros.

Consider Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." That would mean each interval is independently chosen to be real or zero. That would not necessarily produce long contiguous zero blocks; it could produce scattered zeros. Here we have long zero blocks, which could occur with some probability but more likely with a duration-based attack. Attack 2's "random duration" suggests that the attacker switches off for a period, then on for a period, etc. The query day pattern: zeros from index 0 to 39 (40 intervals = 10 hours! 40*15min = 600 min = 10 hours). Then a mix from 40 to 59: some zeros, some non-zero. Then zeros from 60 to 76 (17 intervals = 4.25 hours). Then non-zero mix from 77 to 91, then zeros at the end. So it looks like long off periods with short on periods. That is classic on-off attack (Attack 2). But why are the on periods' values lower than expected? Maybe the reference day is a different day, and the actual consumption on that query day was truly lower? But the mean of the reference week days are all around 100-116, with min around 52-66. The query day's non-zero max is 62.46, which is still lower than the typical min of reference days (except maybe day 5 min 52.13). But the pattern of non-zero values: they peak at 62.46, then later 34.03, etc. It's possible that the on periods capture partial consumption because the meter was only intermittently reporting, or the attacker also scaled down when on. But given the attack definitions, Attack 2 doesn't mention scaling; it says "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." That implies the non-zero samples are untouched. Perhaps in this case, the non-zero samples are actually the original values for that day, and the day genuinely had lower consumption. But the query day is a Friday like reference Day 6, and reference Day 6 had high consumption. But the reference week is earlier in the month (dom=0..6, query dom=13). It's possible that the customer's behavior changed. But the difference is drastic. Compare the non-zero values to the reference week's minimum values: the reference week days have minimums around 52-66. So 62.46 is in the range of possible normal consumption. The non-zero values like 34.03, 24.55, etc., are lower than typical but could be low consumption periods (e.g., night). In reference Day 6, the night values are around 61-104. So 34 is lower but not impossible. However, the query has many zeros, which are abnormal. So it's likely that the attack is Attack 2: the attacker zeroed out certain periods, but the non-zero periods show actual consumption. Could the actual consumption on that day have been that low? Possibly if the customer was away or reduced usage. But given the reference week, the minimum daily mean is 101.11, so a mean of 4.97 is impossible without manipulation. So the attack is definitely present, and the most parsimonious is that the zeros are due to on-off switching (Attack 2), and the non-zero values are whatever consumption occurred during on periods, which might be lower because only some appliances were used or because the attacker also reduced them. But among the given attacks, Attack 2 is the only one that produces long zero blocks. Attack IEEE also produces zeros but without necessarily long blocks; it's per-interval random, so you could still get long blocks by chance, but the probability of 40 consecutive zeros is extremely low if each interval has, say, 50% chance. So Attack 2 is more likely.

We should also consider Attack 4: simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period, all measurements in that period are reduced. If the period is the whole day? No, it says "randomly selected period within the observation window." That would be a single contiguous period, not multiple zero blocks. Attack 6 and 7 don't produce zeros like this. Attack 9 is constant. So Attack 2 seems best.

But wait, the query day also has some very small non-zero values like 0.85, 5.59, 0.85. In reference, the minimum is 61.61. If the on periods are reporting actual consumption, how could there be 0.85? That seems too low. Unless the customer truly had near-zero consumption at those times? In reference, even at night, the lowest is 61.61. So 0.85 is implausible. That suggests that even the non-zero values are manipulated. Attack 3 could produce such small values if the random factor is near zero. Attack 5 would produce completely random values around average * random, which could be small but would not have zeros necessarily. Attack 3 could produce zeros if factor=0, but it doesn't specify if factor can be exactly zero. Usually, "between 0 and 1" might include 0. So an attacker could set some factors to zero, effectively creating zero intervals. But then the long contiguous zeros would require many consecutive factors of exactly zero, which is possible if the attacker chooses them that way. Attack 3 doesn't specify that zero intervals are contiguous; it's per sample, so it could mimic on-off if the attacker sets factors to zero for a block. But the description of Attack 3: "The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." It doesn't say the factors are chosen in blocks; they are random per sample, so zero blocks are unlikely by chance. But an attacker could intentionally choose factors of zero for a duration. However, the description "random factors" suggests they are random, not intentionally set to zero for long periods. So that's less likely.

Attack 2 specifically mentions "on-off attack" where durations of zeros occur. So I'm leaning towards Attack 2, with the caveat that the non-zero values might be the actual consumption during on periods, which happen to be low possibly because the customer's usage was low during those times, or perhaps the attack also includes some scaling but not captured in the pure Attack 2 definition. Given the task, we must choose the single most probable from the list. Attack 2 is the best fit due to long zero blocks.

But let's check Attack IEEE: it says "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." That is per-interval decision, not necessarily contiguous. The query has long contiguous zeros. However, it's possible that the "either reports actual or zero" is randomly applied per interval, and by chance some long zero blocks occur. But 40 consecutive zeros out of 96 intervals: if each interval has a probability p of being zero, and decisions are independent, the probability of 40 consecutive zeros is p^40. Even if p=0.5, that's 9e-13, extremely unlikely. So it's not random independent; it's more likely a duration-based attack. Therefore, Attack 2 is much more plausible.

Thus, classification: Manipulated. Identified Attack: Most Likely Attack 2. Confidence: High. Reasoning: The query day shows long stretches of zero values (first 40 intervals, then later 17 intervals) indicative of an on-off attack where reporting is turned off for random durations. The non-zero intervals show some consumption, but not matching the high consumption pattern of the reference day, possibly due to lower actual usage during on periods or additional scaling, but the defining feature is the zero blocks. Alternative considered: Attack 3 (multiplicative random factors) could produce zeros if factor=0, but unlikely to produce long continuous zero blocks by random per-sample factors. Attack IEEE (per-interval random zeroing) also would not typically yield such extended zero blocks.

The magnitude is drastically reduced due to many zero reports, and the trend is interrupted by flat zero periods. Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Looking at the actual 96 readings for the query day, it is mostly zeros with a few small non-zero values occurring in a short interval (roughly from reading 44 to 60, peaking around 27.26). This pattern is completely inconsistent with the normal consumption of this customer, which typically ranges from around 30 to over 100 and shows clear daily patterns with morning/evening peaks.

Attack 3 would have no exact zeros (unless factor equals zero, but unlikely for many intervals).

Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." That would mean in that selected period, we see reduced values (non-zero), while outside, it's original (non-zero). Here outside is zero, so not.

Attack 5: false values from average * random, no zeros.

Attack 6/7: no long zero stretches.

Attack 12: replacement with lower user's pattern, would be a full day pattern, not zeros.

Thus, none of the defined attacks perfectly match a pattern with both long zero stretches and a brief reduced burst. But Attack 2 is the closest because it introduces zeros for a duration. The fact that the non-zero part is reduced could be due to a partial bypass or combined attack, but the description of Attack 2 says "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." It doesn't specify what happens outside that duration; presumably the original values are reported. In this case, the original values outside the zero duration would be seen, but we see a reduced segment. So if the zero duration is one contiguous block, the non-zero segment would be a separate block. The zero block could be chosen to cover the early morning and late night, with a gap in the middle where consumption is reported. That would yield a profile: zeros for a while, then a block of normal consumption, then zeros again. Here the block of "normal" consumption is far below normal. So if it were Attack 2, we'd expect the non-zero block to be normal; it's not. So Attack 2 alone fails.

Therefore, perhaps the best fit is Attack 2 but with the recognition that the non-zero block is also manipulated, which might be a combination, but the prompt asks for the single most probable attack. Given the available attacks, Attack 2 (on-off) is the only one that produces zeros for extended periods. The reduced non-zero part could be explained if the attacker implemented zeroing but also some reduction (maybe a faulty bypass that doesn't fully report zero, or they zeroed but the meter still records some low level? Not sensible). Alternatively, Attack IEEE could result if intervals are chosen randomly, but the pattern here is contiguous, not random.

Wait, maybe the attacker used Attack IEEE but with a pattern where they substitute zeros for many intervals, but for a contiguous block they report actual? That would not be per-interval independent, but rather a period of actual. Attack IEEE description: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day." The key is "irregular pattern", not continuous blocks. Our query day: long zero block at start, then a short non-zero block, then zero block to end. That is irregular in the sense that zeros dominate, but it's not alternating. It's two zero blocks and one non-zero block. It could be considered irregular. Compared to a typical on-off attack (Attack 2) which is a single contiguous zero period, this is slightly different. But Attack IEEE is more about random zeroing per interval. Given the data, I'd lean toward Attack 2 because the zeroing is essentially for the whole day except a short period; it's like the attacker turned off reporting for most of the day, but maybe there was a brief resumption. Attack 2 could be applied with two zero periods if the attacker chose two durations? But the description says "a random duration" singular.

Let's double-check the statistics: query day mean=1.91, min=0, max=27.26, std=5.12. This indicates most values are zero with a few non-zero. Attack 0 would be all zero, so mean would be 0. Not.

Attack 3: if each sample is multiplied by a random factor 0-1, you would rarely get exact zeros. The presence of exact zeros suggests deliberate zeroing.

Attack 4: "All measurements in that period are reduced" – period could be the whole day, but reduction would still be proportional to original, not zero unless factor is zero.

So the presence of exact zeros for an extended time strongly suggests a zeroing attack. Among those, Attack 2 is the most straightforward. Even though the non-zero values are low, they might be due to a different cause, but perhaps the attacker intended to zero the whole day but the meter recorded some residual consumption (like a small load) due to incomplete bypass. That would be consistent with a physical bypass: the meter is bypassed, reporting zero, but a small amount of current might still flow through the meter, causing sporadic low readings? Not typical; bypass usually results in zero. Alternatively, the short burst could be an attempt to mimic minimal usage, but that would be a false data injection rather than bypass. In that case, Attack 5 might apply if they generate fake low values, but Attack 5 uses average normal consumption * random, so it would produce values around the average, not a burst of low values. Attack 5 would be spread throughout the day. Attack 6/7 produce no exact zeros unless cut-off high. So I'm comfortable that the best match is Attack 2: the attacker replaced consumption with zeros for a random duration, which turned out to be most of the day. The short non-zero block could be the remainder of the day where the attack was not active, but the original consumption was mysteriously low—maybe due to an error, or perhaps the customer genuinely had very low consumption that day? But the reference week shows consistent high consumption, so it's unlikely to be a low-usage day. The fact that the values are low suggests maybe the attacker also manipulated those readings. But within the defined attacks, Attack 2 is the only one that primarily introduces zeros. So I'll go with Attack 2.

Now, confidence: medium, because the non-zero values are not original, which is not fully explained by Attack 2's definition. Alternative considered: Attack IEEE, because it allows some intervals to be zero and some real. But the real intervals should show actual consumption close to normal, which they don't. Attack 0 is ruled out because not all zeros.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.

Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Possible technical causes

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- The attacker has zeroed out all 15‑minute readings for a continuous stretch of about 5.25 hours in the early morning. This eliminates the small but non‑zero overnight consumption that typically appears during that time (as seen in the reference week).
- The on‑off zeroing drastically alters both the trend (creating a flat, zero segment) and the amplitude (reducing total daily consumption and causing an abrupt jump when the meter resumes recording).

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Attack 9: constant average, not constant.

Attack 12: another user's pattern, maybe that other user had zeros at night and lower consumption during day. Possible but without information.

Given the strong signal of a contiguous zero block, Attack 2 is the most characteristic. I think the intended answer is Attack 2, because the zero block is a classic on-off attack. The fact that the rest is lower might be attributed to the customer's typical consumption being lower on that day (maybe due to season or day of month), but the mean 164 vs 515 is drastic, so we might also consider that the on-period is also attacked. But the problem description says: "If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2." So we need to identify the attack type. Could be that the day is a combination of Attack 2 and Attack 1, but again we pick one. In many NTL detection papers, an on-off attack often refers to turning meter off for some periods, but when on, it's normal. The huge drop in mean could be solely due to the zero block if the zero block is long enough. The zero block is from reading 3 to 23, so 21 readings out of 96 (21.875% of day). If the rest were normal with mean around 515, the overall mean would be (0*21 + 515*75)/96 ≈ (38625)/96 ≈ 402.34, which is much higher than 164.67. So the rest must have mean around (164.67*96)/75 ≈ 210.7. So the non-zero part is significantly lower than normal. So the zero block alone doesn't account for the reduction. So clearly, the on period is also manipulated. So the attack is not pure Attack 2.

Maybe Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period" and that period is the entire day? If the period is the whole day, then "All measurements in that period are reduced." So it could cover a case where a period is chosen, and within that period, readings are, say, set to zero or scaled. The attack might be implemented by choosing a start and end, then for each reading in that period, randomly reduce (e.g., multiply by factor). This could produce a contiguous block of zeros if the factor is zero for that block? But Attack 4 might be more like: the attacker reduces consumption by a fixed amount or factor during that period. In the literature, on-off is distinct from other types.

Thus, Attack 7 fails.

Maybe the attack is a combination of Attack 2 (zero block) and Attack 1 (constant factor on the rest). But the problem likely expects us to identify the most prominent one. In many NTL detection challenges, the on-off attack is characterized by zero blocks. The low consumption during the on period might be due to the meter being partially bypassed, which is like a scaling attack, but the problem might consider that as part of the on-off attack if the on-period also is reduced because the bypass remains. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude." Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." This could include the zero block if the period covers those hours, and the rest (outside period) are normal. But again, the rest is not normal.

Attack 5: random values from average. Could produce zeros if the random factor is 0, but again not a block.

Attack 6: cut-off ceiling.

Attack IEEE: random zeroing each interval, could produce blocks but typically scattered.

Thus, none of the attacks exactly match the pattern of a zero block plus heavily reduced on-period. Perhaps the day is actually normal, but the zero block is genuine zero consumption? Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Now we need to classify the attack type. We have definitions:
- Attack 0: all zero.
- Attack 1: constant scaling factor in (0,1) to all readings uniformly reduces magnitude but keeps trend.
- Attack 2: on-off attack, zeros for random duration.
- Attack 3: scaling factor varies per sample (different random factor per reading) - changes trend and amplitude.
- Attack 4: randomly selected period within day, all measurements in that period are reduced. (Could be scaling or zeroing? "reduced" implies could be scaled down, but not necessarily zero). For example, during a period, all readings are multiplied by a factor <1. Changes both trend and amplitude.
- Attack 5: each sample replaced by random value from average consumption * random(0,1). Produces completely different pattern.
- Attack 6: cut-off cap, all values above threshold replaced by threshold.
- Attack 7: subtract threshold, floor at zero.
- Attack 9: flat constant (average daily consumption).
- Attack 10: reversal of pattern.
- Attack 12: replaced by another user's consumption pattern (lower consumer).
- Attack IEEE: for each 15-min interval, either report actual or zero (random selection). Result is irregular pattern with some zero intervals.

In our query day, we see a contiguous block of zeros (9 intervals) and the rest of the day has reduced values but not zero. The non-zero values are not flat, they follow a somewhat realistic shape: increasing to a peak around 480, then dropping, then later another peak. It's not a simple scaling of the reference pattern because the shape is different; the reference day 4 had high consumption early (around 1800s) and later drops to 800-900s and then rises again. The query day's shape is different: it starts moderate (~400s), then gradually decreases, then zeros, then gradual increase to higher values (500s) later. So it's not a simple scaling (Attack 1) because the relative magnitudes are not proportional; the early part of the day in reference was high (~1800) but in query it's ~400s, which would be a scaling factor ~0.22, while the later part in reference was ~1700 and query ~500 scaling factor ~0.29. Not constant. Also the trend is altered, with zeros inserted. So Attack 1 ruled out.

Attack 2 is "on-off attack: replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration". This exactly matches: there is a random duration of zeros (9 consecutive zero samples). The on-off attack often zeros out a contiguous block. So Attack 2 is very plausible.

Attack 3: different random factor per sample, but no zeros. This would produce scaled values throughout, not zeros. Our day has zeros, so that's a key feature. Attack 3 could theoretically produce zeros if the factor is zero for some samples, but it says "random values between 0 and 1" - typically (0,1) continuous, could include zero but usually it's a continuous range and might rarely produce many consecutive zeros. The description says "unique factor per sample" so a few zeros possible, but a block of 9 consecutive zeros is more like an on-off attack, not random per-sample. So Attack 3 less likely.

Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." The period could be reduced but not to zero, typically by a factor. Here we have zeros, not just reduced. So Attack 4 might not produce zeros. It says "reduced" not zeroed. So Attack 4 less likely.

Attack 5: completely random replacement, would not have a contiguous block of zeros and realistic shape elsewhere. So no.

Attack 6: cut-off cap, all above threshold replaced by cap. Would not produce zeros unless threshold is zero (which would be Attack 0 essentially). But here zeros are present, and other values are varying, not capped. So not Attack 6.

Attack 7: subtract threshold, floor at zero. This could produce zeros if values go below threshold. But the pattern: we see a block of zeros, then positive values gradually increasing from near zero. Attack 7 would yield values that are original minus a constant, with zeros where original <= constant. The query day's non-zero values range from 6.10 to 524.62.
