Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning content 
We are asked to determine if the query day (DAY_ID=6) is manipulated or original. The reference week has days 0 through 6, but note that the query day's metadata: DAY_ID=6, dow=5 (Friday), dom=27. The reference week's Day 6 also has DAY_ID=6, dow=5, dom=6. So they are not the same day; the query day is a different day (likely later in the month). The reference week's Day 6 is from the same customer, showing typical Friday behavior. The query day is also a Friday (dow=5) but with dom=27, so a different Friday in December. We should compare the query day's pattern against the typical Friday pattern and the overall week pattern.

First, look at the statistics: Query day mean=681.41, min=0.00, max=1285.88, std=361.17. The max is also lower than typical (1285 vs ~1700). This suggests a reduction or zeroing of consumption.

Now examine the time series. The query day starts with values around 500, then drops to 400s, then suddenly very low values (50, 39, 0) for a stretch of several hours (from around index 10 onward?). So there is a stretch from around 4:45 to 7:00 with mostly zeros, except a 6.43 at 5:15. That's a clear on-off pattern: normal consumption early morning, then a sudden drop to near-zero for a couple hours, then back to moderate consumption. This looks like a targeted zeroing of some intervals, not a constant scaling or complete zeroing.

Now compare to reference week. For the same day-of-week (Friday, Day 6 in reference week), the early morning hours (midnight to 6 AM) show values typically in the 400-600 range, never zero. Day 6 reference: 992.42, 1080.44, 915.49, 849.45, 816.48, 805.49, 596.59, 563.63, 552.64, 706.48, 607.58, 541.54, 497.58, 706.48, 541.54, 464.62, 497.58, 530.55, 497.58, 497.58, 453.63, 486.59, 486.59, 464.62, 464.62, 486.59, 486.59, 453.63, 431.65, 772.53, ... So at the same night/early morning period, the reference day has no zeros, values in 400-500 range. The query day has a sudden drop to 0 after a period of moderate consumption (around 500). This suggests an on-off attack where the meter reports zero for some intervals, while other intervals look relatively normal (though perhaps reduced). Indeed, later in the day, from index 29 onward, values increase to peak around 1285.88 at index 45 (11:15 AM?), which is high but lower than typical peak (1700s). But the presence of zeros and near-zeros is a clear sign of manipulation.

Now which attack type? Attack 0: all zero. Not the case here. Attack 1: multiply by constant random factor between 0 and 1. That would give a uniformly scaled down version of the original consumption, no zeros unless original was zero, but original never has zero. So ruled out. Attack 2: zeros for a random duration. This exactly matches: there is a contiguous block of zeros (and a couple of near-zero values) for about 2-3 hours. The rest of the day has nonzero values, but the overall magnitude might be somewhat reduced (maybe partially due to Attack 2 or additional scaling?). The query day's max is 1285.88, while typical Friday max is ~1707. So some reduction exists in non-zero periods, but that could be due to different usage or a secondary scaling. Attack 2 is defined as replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. That's essentially an on-off attack. The query day shows a clear "off" period with zeros. Note there is also a single 6.43 value amidst zeros, which could be a slight deviation or maybe a not perfect zero, but the description of Attack 2 allows random duration of zeros, but doesn't say all samples in that duration must be zero; maybe the attacker attempted to zero but some residual? Or it could be Attack 4: energy theft over a randomly selected period, with measurements reduced. Attack 4 says "All measurements in that period are reduced." Not necessarily to zero. So if the period is reduced to zero, that would be a subset of Attack 4? But Attack 2 explicitly is "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." The query day has a clear block of zeros from index 19 to 28, and some near-zeros before that. So Attack 2 seems likely.

Other attacks: Attack 3 multiplies by different random factors per sample, would not produce exact zeros unless factor 0, but could produce zeros if the factor includes 0, but the description says "multiplies the actual consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1", so it could produce zeros if the random variable is 0, but that would be an extreme coincidence for several consecutive intervals. Not likely. Attack 4 reduces measurements over a random period, but not necessarily to zero; the reduction could be any amount. Here we see reduction to exactly zero for many intervals, which is more specific to Attack 2. Attack 5 replaces each sample by false value from multiplying average normal consumption by random variable, would produce values unrelated to original pattern, but may not produce zeros unless the random variable is 0, but unlikely. There are zeros but also other values that seem to follow a plausible pattern (morning ramp-up). Attack 5 would produce completely different pattern, not something that still partially follows the original shape. Attack 6 puts a cap, Attack 7 subtracts a cut-off. Neither would produce zeros unless the cut-off is high and subtraction yields negative then zero, but that would cause many low values and zero for those that fall below cut-off, but the pattern would show a shift. Not likely. Attack 9 constant average, not true. Attack 10 reversal, no. Attack 12 substitution with another user, might have zeros but not likely. Attack IEEE: for each 15-minute interval, either actual or zero. That also could produce zeros arbitrarily across the day. But the query day has a contiguous block of zeros, which is more like an on-off attack over a duration, rather than sporadic zero intervals. Attack IEEE says "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." That could result in any pattern of zeros, including a contiguous block if by chance many consecutive intervals are chosen to be zero. But Attack 2 specifically mentions "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration," implying a single contiguous block (or maybe multiple blocks?). The description says "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack." So it's a single duration (one continuous period). The query day has one main zero block (maybe with a tiny blip of 6.43). That matches Attack 2 better than IEEE which would have independent decisions per interval, making a contiguous block of that length less likely. Additionally, Attack IEEE is a specific named attack from the IEEE dataset, but Attack 2 is the classic on-off. The query day's pattern is classic on-off: normal then suddenly off for a while, then back on. Attack 2 fits well.

Now, is there any other evidence? The query day's nonzero periods still seem somewhat lower than reference Friday. For instance, early morning (midnight to 2 AM): reference Day 6 has values around 800-1000, query has 500-600. So there might be some overall reduction even in "on" periods. But Attack 2 only zeros out a duration, it doesn't scale other values. However, the query day is a different Friday, and consumption could vary. The mean of query day (681) is much lower than any reference day, which all have means >1100. The min of 0 and the extended zero block strongly indicate manipulation. Attack 2 alone would lower the mean significantly, but would not affect non-zero periods unless there is additional scaling. But the non-zero periods could be normal for that day; the customer might simply use less electricity on that particular Friday. The extreme low of 0 is the red flag. So the attack is Attack 2, on-off.

We should confirm if any other attack could produce zeros in a block. Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period. All measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't say to zero; it could be any reduction. So it's less specific. Attack 2 explicitly zeros. Attack IEEE could also produce zeros in a block if by chance, but contiguous block is not its defining characteristic. Attack 2 is the classic on-off. I'll go with Attack 2.

Confidence: High, because the presence of a contiguous zero block is classic on-off, and the reference day has no such pattern. The week shows typical usage never drops to zero.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: The attacker replaced electricity consumption readings with zeros for a continuous period of approximately five hours at the start of the day. After that zero block, consumption returns to a pattern that roughly resembles a daily load curve but with a noticeably lower peak and average than any day in the reference week.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The mean daily consumption drops from a reference week average of ~138–143 kWh to only 63.03 kWh, and the maximum decreases from >200 to 118.90 kWh. - Possible technical causes: Full meter bypass for a portion of the day, false data injection that substitutes zero readings during certain intervals, or reverse current flow that causes the meter to record zero consumption temporarily.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

After the zero period, consumption resumes but at a lower peak than typical, which could be due to partial bypass or other factors, but consistent with the on-off attack where the meter is bypassed for a random duration, then reports actual (or possibly reduced) values. The technical causes include full meter bypass, false data injection, or reverse current during that period.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

**Classification:** Manipulated

**Identified Attack:**  
- Most Likely: Attack 2  
- Confidence: Medium  
- Reasoning: The query day contains long continuous blocks of exactly zero readings—26 samples (~6.5 h) from midnight and 5 samples (~1.25 h) at the end—which are never present in the reference week (min values always ≥1.75). This matches the on‑off attack definition (Attack 2) where consumption is replaced by zeros for a certain duration.  
- Top alternatives considered and ruled out:  
  - Attack IEEE: Describes per‑interval random zero substitution, but the zeros here are clustered in two long, contiguous segments rather than scattered irregularly across the full day, making a deliberate bypass more likely.  
  - Attack 7: Subtraction of a cut‑off would produce zeros only for values below the cut‑off, but the resulting non‑zero values after subtraction do not align with the query day’s pattern (e.g., early morning values would remain non‑zero after subtraction, but the query shows zeros).  

**Attack_Behavior_Explanation:**  
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: The attacker selectively reports zero for extended periods (e.g., nighttime and early morning), while the remaining intervals still show consumption but may be somewhat reduced compared to the reference Friday.  
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Mean daily consumption drops from ~10.69 to 5.48; the trend gains flat zero segments that replace the usual low‑but‑positive nighttime baseline, while the daytime trend retains a roughly normal shape but with suppressed peaks.  
- Possible technical causes: Intermittent full meter bypass, false data injection, or reverse current applied during specific hours to hide usage.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Original | Manipulated

- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week
- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes

Given the complexity, maybe it's Attack 12: replacing with a lower-consuming user's pattern. The query day pattern looks like a typical consumption pattern with low overnight usage, a morning ramp, a midday peak, and evening decline, but overall lower magnitude except a spike around 10:45. Could it be that the attacker used another user's data? Without seeing other users, we can't be sure, but Attack 12 is defined as replacing their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. That would result in a pattern that still looks like a legitimate (but lower) consumption profile. The query day pattern is not completely abnormal; it has a typical shape with night zeros (many households do have near-zero usage at night). However, the reference week includes Day 0 and Day 6 (Sunday and Saturday) which have very low consumption (min ~2.33, max ~3.49, mean ~2.78). Those days show a flat low usage, not zero. The query day has zeros. That's unusual for this customer: the normal minimum consumption is around 2.33 or 1.75, never zero. So the presence of zeros is a strong indicator of manipulation. Attack 12 would not normally introduce zeros unless the lower-consuming user also had zeros. But the reference week's low-usage days don't have zeros, so if the attacker used a lower-consuming user, that user probably also had non-zero night consumption. So zeros are not explained by Attack 12.

Attack 4: reduction over a random period. Again, zeros could be if the reduction factor was 1 (total reduction) for some intervals. But Attack 4 is described as "All measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't specify to zero, just reduced. The query day has many zeros, so Attack 2 is more explicit about zeros.

Could it be Attack 5 with random values? Attack 5 multiplies average normal consumption (which is around 10.69) by a random number between 0 and 1, giving values between 0 and 10.69. But we have values up to 15.95, which is above the average normal consumption. So Attack 5 wouldn't produce values above the average unless the random factor can be >1? It says "multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1," so max is average, which is 10.69. Query max is 15.95, so that's ruled out.

Attack 3 multiplies each sample by different random factors 0-1, reducing the original. Original max 21.54 multiplied by a factor could yield 15.95 if factor ~0.74. However, zeros would only occur if the original was zero, which it wasn't. So Attack 3 cannot explain zeros.

Thus, the only attacks that can produce zeros are Attack 2, Attack 7 (if original value <= cut-off, becomes zero), and maybe Attack IEEE. Attack 4 could also result in zeros if the reduction amount equals the original value, but it's less explicit.

Now, compare Attack 2 and Attack 7. Attack 7 subtracts a cut-off and sets negative to zero. That would produce zeros for all values below the cut-off. In reference Day 5, early morning values are around 2.91 constant. If cut-off was >2.91, say 3.0, then those become zero. Then later values would be reduced by about 3.0. The max 21.54 - 3.0 = 18.54, but query max is 15.95. So not a simple subtraction. Could be a cut-off of 5.59? 21.54 - 5.59 = 15.95. But early morning values are 2.91, so they'd become negative (2.91-5.59 = -2.68) => zero. So a cut-off of 5.59 would zero out all values <=5.59. In reference Day 5, many values are below 5.59: indices 0-24 mostly 2.91, so zeros. Then indices 25-32? 25:4.66, 26:5.24, 27:5.24, 28:5.82, 29:6.40, 30:6.98, 31:8.15, etc. 4.66 would be zero, 5.24 zero, 5.24 zero, 5.82 -> 0.23, 6.40 -> 0.81, 6.98 -> 1.39, etc. Let's check query day at those indices: 25:0.00 (ref 4.66 -> zero, matches), 26:0.24 (ref 5.24 -> 5.24-5.59 negative, zero but query has 0.24, mismatch), 27:0.00 (ref 5.24 -> zero, matches), 28:0.82 (ref 5.82 -> 5.82-5.59=0.23, query 0.82, not match). So subtraction doesn't produce consistent mapping.

Attack 2, on the other hand, sets a random duration to zero. The query day has a block of zeros from indices 0-25 (26 samples, 6.5 hours) and another block at indices 91-95 (5 samples, 1.25 hours). That's two blocks, not one contiguous duration. But maybe the attacker applied the on-off attack with a schedule: e.g., turned off from midnight to 6:30am, then later turned off again from 11:15pm to midnight. That's still "on-off" but multiple periods. The definition says "a random duration" singular, but in practice, an attacker might do it multiple times. I'll check Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." That could produce any pattern of zeros, including long blocks. The description says "creating an irregular pattern across the full day." If it were entirely random per interval, you'd expect zeros scattered throughout, like a checkerboard. Instead, we have long runs of zeros, which is more consistent with a deliberate off period. I'd lean towards Attack 2, possibly with the understanding that the "random duration" could encompass non-contiguous periods if the attacker toggles the meter bypass multiple times. But to be precise, let's see if any attack explicitly fits multiple zero blocks. Attack 4: energy theft over a randomly selected period, but it reduces, not necessarily zeros. Attack 2 is the only one that says "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." It doesn't say "contiguous", but "duration" usually implies a single continuous interval. Still, it's the closest.

Another angle: The mean of the query day is roughly half, and the non-zero period looks like a scaled-down version with some timing shift. Could it be Attack 1 with constant factor but the factor is not constant across the day due to the zeros? No, Attack 1 multiplies all samples by a constant 0-1, so no zeros unless original zero.

Given that, I think the most likely attack is Attack 2. Let's verify by analyzing the non-zero period in query day against reference day with a possible shift. Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.

Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.

We are given the task to determine if the query day is attacked or original, and if attacked, identify the attack type and explain the behavior. We have per-day statistics and the full 96 readings for the reference week (7 days) and the query day.

First, let's examine the query day: 96 readings (15-minute intervals) for one day. The per-day statistics: min=0.00, max=99.40, std=37.38, mean=37.49. The reference week consists of 7 days with means ranging from 121.59 to 140.38, all much higher. The query day mean is 37.49, which is drastically lower than any day in the reference week. The maximum on the query day is 99.40, which is lower than the minimum daily maximum of the reference week (which is 197.80 on Day 0). So the magnitude is significantly reduced. Let's map: 96 intervals, 15 min each, so interval 0 is 00:00-00:15, interval 1 is 00:15-00:30, etc. Up to interval 95 is 23:45-24:00. So there is a long period of zeros. That suggests an on-off type attack or partial zeroing. The reference week days show no such long zero periods; they have continuous consumption, typically low at night, higher during day. So this is clearly manipulated.

Now, which attack type? Let's review the attack definitions:

- Attack 0: All samples replaced with zero for entire duration. Here not all zeros; there are non-zero values later. So not Attack 0.
- Attack 1: Multiplies by constant random factor between 0 and 1. That would preserve the shape but reduce magnitude proportionally. The query day does not look proportionally scaled from any reference day; it has zeros and a different temporal pattern. The reference week days have a typical two-peak pattern: morning peak and evening peak, with moderate consumption in between. For example, Day 0 (Sunday): starts high 137, then drops, then low around 30, then increases to 56, later high around 194 in evening. The query day: starts with 15, drops to zeros for a long stretch, then later spikes up to 99. The pattern is not simply scaled. So not Attack 1.
- Attack 2: Replaces consumption samples with zeros for a random duration (on-off attack). That matches: there is a long zero segment from roughly 1:00 am to 10:00 am? Actually intervals: index 3 (0:45?) to index 40 (10:00?) all zeros. That's a contiguous block of zeros. Then later non-zero values. So Attack 2 fits.
- Attack 3: Multiplies by different random factors per sample. The query day has zeros, but Attack 3 scales each sample by a random factor between 0 and 1, so it would not produce exact zeros unless the original value was zero (unlikely) or factor is zero (but factor between 0 and 1, not inclusive of zero? "between (0,1)" typically means >0 and <1, so not zero). So no zeros. Also the pattern would be a noisy version of the original shape, but it would not create long zero stretches. So Attack 3 unlikely.
- Attack 4: Energy theft over a randomly selected period; all measurements in that period are reduced. Not necessarily zeroed; "reduced" could mean multiplied by a factor. However, the query day has many zeros, not just reduced. Attack 4 could be a reduction, but typically it's multiplying by a factor <1 for a continuous interval. If the factor is zero, that would be zeroing, but then it's essentially like Attack 2. However, Attack 2 explicitly zeros for a random duration. Attack 4 says "All measurements in that period are reduced." It does not specifically say zeroed. Given the definition, Attack 2 explicitly generates zeros for a duration, which matches better.
- Attack 5: Replaces each sample by false value = average normal consumption * random(0,1). That would produce completely random values with mean around half of average consumption, but no zeros necessarily and no typical shape. The query day shows a pattern that resembles a consumption pattern but with a big gap of zeros. It's not random numbers; after the zeros, it has a rising and falling pattern that might be similar to a consumption peak. So not Attack 5.
- Attack 6: Cut-off point: all consumption values above it replaced by that cut-off. Would produce values capped at a certain maximum. The query day has zeros, not capped values, so not Attack 6.
- Attack 7: Subtract a cut-off point, negative -> zero. That could produce zeros for low values and reduced values for others. That might produce a stretch of zeros if the original consumption was below the cut-off. For example, if cut-off is high, then many values become zero. The query day has a long zero stretch at night when consumption is normally low (around 30). If a high cut-off is subtracted, those 30-ish values go to zero, and higher values later might be reduced. That could be possible. Let's check: The reference week days have night-time minimums around 27-30. But here they are small positives. So Attack 7 would produce zeros for values below cut-off. In the reference days, night values are consistently around 27-30, so if cut-off is above 30, all become zero. But then the early morning values that are lower? - Attack 9: All samples replaced by average daily energy consumption. Flat constant profile. Not this.
- Attack 10: Reverses consumption pattern without reducing total amount. Here total amount is severely reduced, not just reversed. So no.
- Attack 12: Replaces pattern with lower-consuming user. That would be a different shape, possibly lower magnitude, but not necessarily zeros. The query day has zeros; it's unlikely to exactly match another user's pattern with long zero gaps.
- Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval, either reports actual or substitutes zero, across the entire day. That creates an irregular on/off pattern of zeros and actual values scattered throughout the day. The query day has a contiguous block of zeros (from about 1am to 10am), then later non-zero values, but also some zeros within non-zero region (like index 44 is 0.00). Attack IEEE would have a pattern where individual intervals are randomly zeroed, not a long contiguous block; it says "the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero" for each interval. That could produce a stretch of zeros if many consecutive intervals are chosen to be zeroed, but it would be random. However, the definition says "some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day." The query day shows a clear contiguous block of zeros for 38 intervals, which is not just random intervals; it's a systematic on-off attack (Attack 2) where a continuous period is set to zero. Attack 2 explicitly says "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration". That matches perfectly.

Thus, the most likely attack is Attack 2 (on-off attack). Let's consider alternatives: Attack 7 could be considered because subtracting a cut-off could zero out low values, but the first three non-zero values are very low (15,11,7) while original night values are high (137,133,126). So if cut-off were, say, 122, then 137-122=15, 133-122=11, 126-122=4 (but we have 7.83, close). Then later, after the zero block, we see values like 73.76, 77.42, etc. Let's test: Day 0 original evening values are around 194. If cut-off 122, then 194-122=72, which matches the 70s. But Day 0 original early morning (around 00:00-00:45) are 137,133,126. With cut-off 122: 15,11,4. So the first three readings match approximately: 15.15, 11.49, 7.83 (maybe rounding). Then from interval 3 onward, Day 0 original values: 49.08, 41.03, 37.36, 36.63, 35.16, 33.70, 33.70, 32.23, 30.77, 31.50, 31.50, 30.77, 30.04, 30.77, 30.04, 30.77, 29.30, 30.04, 30.04, 30.77, 30.04, 35.90, 56.41, 54.21, 42.49, 43.22, 42.49, 41.76, 42.49, 42.49, 27.11, 34.43, 65.20, 63.00, 82.05, 82.78, 82.78, 83.52, 143.59... If we subtract 122, all those values become negative or zero (since max early morning is 56), so they'd all be reported as zero. That matches the long zero block from index 3 to about index 40. Indeed, index 3 is 0:45? Original Day 0 values after first three: 49.08 (<122) becomes zero. So all would be zero until values exceed 122. Not matching well. Actually, query day index 44 is 0.00, which would correspond to original 142.86-122=20.86, not zero. So not a simple subtraction. So Attack 7 is less likely.

Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." This might be similar to Attack 2 if reduction is to zero, but Attack 4 doesn't specify zeroing, just reduction. However, the definition says "reduced", not "zeroed". So typical reduction is scaling. Attack 2 explicitly zeroes. The presence of exact zeros argues for Attack 2.

Attack IEEE: If each interval has a probability of being zeroed, we could get a block of zeros if many consecutive zeros are chosen. But the probability of 38 consecutive zeros randomly is extremely low unless the probability of zeroing is very high. Attack IEEE is more of a random on/off per interval, not a single continuous duration. Attack 2 explicitly says "for a random duration", implying a contiguous period. So Attack 2 fits better.

Therefore, the most likely attack is Attack 2.

Now, we need to provide output: Classification: Manipulated; Most Likely: Attack 2; Confidence: High; Reasoning: compare and rule out Attack 7 and Attack IEEE; then Attack_Behavior_Explanation.

Let's structure the reasoning carefully.

First, note the drastic reduction in mean (from ~120-140 to 37.5) and the presence of a long zero stretch that is atypical for this customer. Rule out Attack 0 (not all zero), Attack 1 (not scaled shape, zeros present), Attack 3 (no random scaling, zeros exist), Attack 5 (not random values around average), Attack 6 (no cap pattern), Attack 9 (not constant), Attack 10 (not reversed), Attack 12 (not another user's pattern with zeros). Then focus on Attack 2 vs Attack 7 vs Attack IEEE.

For Attack 2: The query day shows a contiguous block of zero values from interval 4 to interval 40, which is a random duration of zeroing, typical of on-off attack. The non-zero segments appear to be actual consumption values (not reduced or capped), as they show a plausible consumption pattern (e.g., afternoon peak). This matches Attack 2's mechanism: replace with zero for a random period.

For Attack 7: This would require a constant subtraction. If we try to find a cut-off value such that subtracting it from a reference day yields zeros for low values and positive for high, the non-zero values do not match any single reference day consistently. For instance, using Day 0 with cut-off around 122, the first three values match but later values are inconsistent (e.g., query index 42 is 34.93 vs expected 64.81). Thus, not a consistent subtraction. Moreover, Attack 7 states "does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption," but the query day's trend is significantly altered due to the zero block, so it's not just a vertical shift.

For Attack IEEE: This attack creates an irregular pattern of zero and actual intervals across the full day. The query day has a single contiguous zero interval rather than scattered zeroes, implying a deliberate on-off attack rather than per-interval random substitution. Moreover, the zero block spans 38 consecutive intervals, which is highly improbable under a random per-interval scheme.

Thus, Attack 2 is the best match.

This manipulation greatly reduces both the magnitude (daily mean drops) and alters the trend by eliminating the morning and early afternoon consumption. Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. 2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Output Format:

- Original | Manipulated

Identified Attack:  (fill only if manipulated)

- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

Attack types:
Attack 0: all zero - not the case.
Attack 1: multiply by constant (0,1) - would keep same pattern with reduced magnitude; here we have zeros, so not constant multiplication.
Attack 2: on-off attack: zeros for random durations. The query day has long stretches of zeros, especially the first ~30 readings after the first few, and the last few. This looks like an on-off attack, where the attacker turns off reporting for certain periods, resulting in zero readings, while other periods show normal (or reduced?) consumption. Check: Attack 2 "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." The query day has zeros for a significant portion (readings 3-33 except reading 5=43.49, and readings 93-96). So about 32 readings early on and 4 at end are zero. That's a random duration? It could be a specific time-based attack. The definition says "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration". That fits: the attacker zeroed out large chunks. Could this be Attack IEEE? Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day." That is also similar: random intervals zeroed. But Attack 2 is described as "on-off attack" and "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration", meaning a continuous block of zeros? "for a random duration" suggests a single continuous period of zeros, not multiple scattered intervals. The query day has multiple zero blocks: a long one at the beginning (with a single brief nonzero at 43.49) and a short one at the end. That's more like multiple instances of zeroing. Attack IEEE explicitly says "For each 15-minute interval... either reports actual or zero", implying independent random substitution per interval, leading to a possibly scattered pattern of zeros. In the query day, the zeros are mostly contiguous except for a single nonzero (43.49) in the middle of the zero stretch. That looks like a large contiguous block but with a small glitch. So it could be Attack 2 with a long duration (most of the morning) and a short block at the end. But is the nonzero 43.49 an anomaly? Possibly the attacker's mechanism had a brief failure? The definition of Attack 2: "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." That suggests a single continuous period of zeros. The query day has multiple separated zero periods, so it might be Attack IEEE, which is more granular. However, Attack IEEE is described as: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day." That would mean zeros could appear anywhere intermittently. In the query day, we see a long block of zeros from reading 3 to 33 (except reading 5), and then zeros at 93-96. That's not completely scattered; it's blocky. So it might be Attack 2 with two separate durations? Attack 2 says "for a random duration" (singular), but could be multiple blocks if the attack is repeated. The description: "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current." Usually, on-off attack means switching between real and zero for periods. So it could be multiple on-off cycles. The query day has a long off period, then on, then off at the end. So that fits on-off.

Also consider Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." That reduces, not zeros. Attack 6 and 7 involve cut-off values, which would not produce zeros unless all values are below cut-off? Attack 7 subtracts a cutoff and sets negative to zero, so could produce zeros, but likely not for many readings if original is high. Attack 5: each sample replaced by false value from average normal * random(0,1) - would cause random values, not zeros. Attack 12: replaced with lower-consuming user's pattern - would not have zeros per se. Attack 10: reverses pattern - not this. Attack 9: constant - no. Attack 3: multiply by varying factors - would not produce zeros unless factor is zero, but factor is between 0 and 1, so zero is possible if factor is zero? Attack 3: "multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." It could produce zeros if the factor is exactly 0? But random factors typically not exactly zero, they are in (0,1) open interval? The description says between 0 and 1, maybe inclusive? Usually, attacks 1 and 3 use between (0,1) meaning strictly positive, not zero. So zeros are unlikely. Attack 2 explicitly replaces with zeros. Attack IEEE also uses zero substitution.

Thus, the most likely is either Attack 2 or Attack IEEE. Which one? Attack IEEE says "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." That would imply that each 15-min reading is independently either actual or zero. In the query day, we have a long contiguous block of zeros from reading 3 to 33 (with one exception). That block of 31 zeros out of 31 readings (except one) is highly unlikely if each reading independently had a probability of zero. It's more likely a continuous off period. The single nonzero reading at position 5 (43.49) could be a glitch or maybe it's part of the normal pattern? In the reference Sunday, at the same time of day? We need to align time: 96 readings from midnight. Reading 1 is 00:00-00:15, reading 2 is 00:15-00:30, etc. So the query day starts with 429.85 at midnight? Compared to reference Sunday Day 0: 1598.48 at reading 1. That's much lower. So the attack likely started early. The block of zeros from reading 3 (00:30-00:45) to reading 33 (08:00-08:15) is a continuous 7.5-hour block of zeros, with a brief spike of 43.49 at reading 5 (01:00-01:15). That 43.49 could be a misreported value or a small consumption. So essentially, the morning is zeroed out. Then readings 34-92 are non-zero (except maybe some near end are zero). Then readings 93-96 (23:00-00:00) are zero. So that is two distinct zero blocks: one long morning block and a short late-night block. That's a classic on-off attack with two off periods. Attack IEEE would likely produce a more random distribution of zeros throughout the day, not large contiguous chunks. Therefore, Attack 2 is more fitting.

But also consider Attack 7: subtract cut-off and zero negative. Could produce zeros if consumption is below cut-off. But the original morning consumption in the reference Sunday was high (around 1500 at start), so subtracting a cutoff would not yield zero unless cutoff is very high. Not likely.

Check Attack 0: no, because there are non-zero values.

Thus, I'll classify as Manipulated, most likely Attack 2 (on-off attack). Confidence: High, because the pattern of contiguous zero blocks is characteristic. Alternatives: Attack IEEE (random zero scattering, but the contiguous blocks argue against it) and Attack 7 (cut-off subtraction, but would produce zeros only when consumption < cutoff, not a full morning block). I'll mention those.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: The attacker temporarily disables consumption reporting, forcing the meter to output zeros for extended periods (e.g., during early morning and late evening). Outside the zero intervals, the meter reports actual (or possibly slightly reduced) consumption, resulting in a pattern with chunks of normal behavior and chunks of zero.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The overall daily mean drops significantly (from ~1957 to ~978). The trend is disrupted – normal morning activity is replaced by a flat zero segment, and the usual evening decline is cut short by a zero tail. The remaining non‑zero intervals still show a recognizable load shape (morning ramp‑up, midday peak, afternoon plateau) but with lower peak values.
- Possible technical causes: This attack can be executed via a physical meter bypass (e.g., a switch that disconnects the meter for part of the day), magnetic interference that blanks readings, reverse current injection, or false data injection that overwrites selected time blocks with zero.
